2.45 pm

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Education and Skills (Lord Adonis): This has been a very useful debate on the important issue of the quality of training and qualification, which members of the childcare workforce attain. We attach
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC295
 
great importance to quality and training. It is central to our policy, hence Clause 13, which imposes duties on local authorities in relation to training.

I pick up the amendments in the name of the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley, in respect of accreditation. There are standards now about qualifications which those who work in under-fives settings are required to achieve. The annex to my letter of 2 May to the noble Lord, Lord Northbourne, sets out the existing standards and the standards we will expect those working in such settings to attain after the measures in the Bill and other reforms have taken effect.

We want to see steadily more highly trained professional staff working in our under-fives settings. An essential part of the 10-year childcare strategy is the transformation fund, to which we have made repeated reference in these debates. Some £125 million a year between 2006 and 2008 will go towards improving quality, without providers having to pass those costs on to parents.

The stringent duty in Clause 13 on local authorities to secure the provision of training for childcare providers is a crucial part of the Bill. I circulated a paper to the Committee entitled Childcare Bill: Duty to Secure Sufficient Childcare; it went out in the batch of 30 March to the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, and was copied to others. It set out our expectations on the information, advice and training that local authorities must secure for providers. Paragraph 35 on page 6 shows that we are clear about the duties that we intend to set out. It says:

    "Regulations and guidance will indicate that local authorities should ensure that providers have access to information, advice and training on",

followed by a list of specific areas where they are required to ensure that providers have access to training. Those are: Ofsted registration procedures; delivery of the early years foundation stage; the Ofsted Childcare Register; safeguarding and child protection, which I know the noble Baronesses, Lady Morris and Lady Walmsley, will refer to later; inclusion; the Common Assessment Framework; and business skills. It adds:

    "Local authorities will be expected to work with a range of organisations to secure the information, advice and training that childcare providers need rather than provide it all directly".

So their role is a commissioning role as well as that of a direct provider.

I believe that the statements we have made about how we intend to take forward the duty to secure sufficient childcare, including the duties in relation to information, advice and training, meet a number of the points that have been raised.

We are currently considering the detailed requirements of the early years foundation stage and the Ofsted Childcare Register including, for example, how we might require that a certain proportion of practitioners in each early years setting are qualified to levels 3 and 2, and what requirements should be placed on providers in terms of training in areas such as first aid and child protection.
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC296
 

While many courses that childcare practitioners undertake lead to qualifications that are accredited in some way, some are not. That does not mean that they are not good and effective courses that address the needs of the childcare sector. For example, some local authorities choose to devise their own pre-registration courses for childminders rather than buying in off-the-peg ones so that they can tailor them to meet the specific needs of childminders in their area. For example, local authorities may have a number of individuals who expressed an interest in childminding who have English as a second language and would prefer courses to meet their particular ESOL needs. In areas such as those, local authorities are right to consider providing their own courses rather than off-the-peg courses provided by others. Since we want to ensure that there is proper flexibility for local authorities to meet needs, we do not believe it right that we should over-regulate them in the way we think would occur if we accepted the amendments.

Requiring local authorities, by law, to use accredited training only would, we believe, impose unacceptable rigidities and restrictions upon them. It would also increase the cost of providing such training, whether or not that cost was value for money, meaning more of the cost being passed on to childcare providers and trainees.

Taking up the point made by the noble Baroness, Lady Howarth, in previous debates, we need to bear it in mind that many practitioners working in childcare will have gained their qualifications some time ago, before current accreditation arrangements were in place. We would not wish to establish new requirements that inadvertently disregarded those qualifications or imposed an expectation that such experienced practitioners would have to requalify.

Furthermore, the amendments would be rather difficult to implement in practice, as they do not specify who the qualifications and training should be accredited by. If there were a satisfactory route through which some courses could be accredited, we believe that would be unlikely to cover them all.

Turning to the noble Earl's idea of introducing mandatory in-service training days or afternoons following the model of INSET days in schools, which is the intention behind his Amendment No. 73A, we entirely agree with him that high quality training is crucial to ensure that we have a highly skilled workforce that can help our children achieve the best possible outcomes. The early years foundation stage welfare requirements will include requirements for qualification levels, and I have sent a note to Members of the Committee on some of our proposals in this area. They are set out in the annex to my letter of 2 May to the noble Lord, Lord Northbourne.

We would strongly encourage early years practitioners and providers to engage fully in ongoing professional development, including training days and sessions as appropriate. But we do not believe that a statutory requirement for practitioners to take days or afternoons away from settings would be the right course, because we fear the rigidities that this would
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC297
 
introduce into the provision, including the adverse effects on the children whose provision would be interrupted thereby. Instead, we believe it should be for providers and managers to ensure that their employees have access to training, including that secured by the local authority. Providers are best placed to balance such training arrangements and the consequential impact on staffing with the needs of children attending their setting.

Local authorities are already encouraged to provide opportunities for early years practitioners to undertake continuous professional development in any event. The current Sure Start guidance says:

    "We expect local authorities to continue to promote, fund and deliver training and development programmes in order to ensure that Foundation Stage practitioners receive an average of 4 days training and development per practitioner, per year",

which I think is the kind of level of training provision the noble Earl has in mind in his amendment on INSET days. We will continue to guide local authorities and providers in this way, but we do not consider that a statutory requirement for in-service training would be in the best interests of early years providers, their staff or the children concerned.

I turn to the issue of pre-registration training, covered by Amendment No. 64 tabled by the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley. We believe that to insist that pre-registration is undertaken by childminders prior to registration in all cases would act as a disincentive to some to apply for registration, and may result in valuable childcare places being lost. We have looked carefully at the key issues impacting on the recruitment and retention of childminders, particularly the impact of Ofsted's regulatory role, and the length of time taken to be registered and start working is already, without such a requirement, identified as a key disincentive among those suitable to come forward. Childminders said that they had set out to start childminding as soon as possible, for obvious reasons—the need to start earning a wage was important to them—and of course many childminders operate only for a short period of time while they are at home looking after their own young children.

We intend to carry forward the existing requirements that wherever possible childminders should complete a local-authority-approved registration course prior to being registered, and it is an existing requirement that such courses must be completed within six months of becoming registered. That requirement is effectively policed, because Ofsted inspects all newly registered providers seven months after registration, and the training of childminders is a key part of that inspection. Furthermore, regulations under Clause 35 will set out requirements for registration. We have made available a paper to illustrate the matters we expect to include in those regulations. As well as setting a requirement that, in order to be registered, an applicant and their premises must be suitable for the care of young children, the regulations will enable Ofsted to take into account the likelihood that the applicant will be able to meet the learning, development and welfare requirements of the
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC298
 
EYFS once registered. Childminders will therefore not be able to be registered if, in the view of Ofsted, they will not be able to deliver the EYFS to an appropriate standard.

I believe that we have sought to address the concerns raised by noble Lords in Committee today, but we are not minded to further amendments that we believe would introduce unfortunate rigidities into the system that would not be to the advantage of the workforce or the children concerned.

4 May 2006 : Column GC303
 
whether Parliament should stipulate, as a matter of national law, that it should be free of charge. We do not think it right to stipulate in all cases that it should be free. Turning to Amendment No. 53 tabled by the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, concerning the duty to secure "compliance with child protection requirements", we share entirely her concerns but believe that they have been met within the regime we are putting in place both for the early-years foundation stage and for the implementation of the Safeguarding Vulnerable Groups Bill. Under the welfare requirements within the early-years foundation stage, providers will be required to take necessary steps to safeguard and promote the welfare of children in their care. These steps will include having in place a child protection policy that reflects the advice given in the government document, What to do if you are worried a child is being abused, and ensuring that all staff are aware of the procedures approved by the local safeguarding children board about the reporting of suspected abuse. As of last month, when they were established nation-wide, local safeguarding children boards have been required to consider and agree how the relevant organisations in each local area will co-operate to safeguard children in that locality and to ensure the effectiveness of what they do. This will include making sure that training on safeguarding and promoting the welfare of children is provided in order to meet local needs.

As I mentioned earlier, Clause 13 enhances existing duties to require local authorities to secure the provision of advice and information about childminding and day care, and training for people who provide these services. I can tell the Committee that the regulations and guidance under this duty will reflect the new welfare requirements which all early years providers will have to meet, including in respect of safeguarding. The regulations will specifically require local authorities to secure information, advice and training to support childcare providers in complying with local child protection procedures as approved by the relevant local safeguarding children board. We will also be publishing guidance following the passage of the Safeguarding Vulnerable Groups Bill to make sure that organisations and individuals are aware of their responsibilities under the new vetting and barring scheme.

Lord Adonis: I shall respond now to the issues affecting Wales. The Welsh national minimum standards, which are applicable to registered providers of childcare for children under the age of eight in Wales, require providers to operate to a satisfactory standard and to keep children safe. This means that they need to undertake the training required in order to fulfil this obligation. Such training will include training in child protection.

In addition, the Welsh national minimum standards require providers to have in place, at the point of application for registration, a statement setting out their own child protection policy. This statement should be based on the procedures of their local safeguarding children boards. Clause 23 of the Bill provides that a Welsh local authority may assist any person who provides childcare, and subsection (2) provides that this may include financial assistance—which, of course, could include the funding of training. 4 May 2006 : Column GC307
 
of being given at little or no cost to parents, and has been shown in many cases to have advantages for the child over professional care in so far as it reinforces the network of security and trust provided by a functional family. Do the Government intend to make kinship carers feel valued as a key element in the national childcare team, or do they not? Do they intend to help and empower kinship carers to do a good job in that respect?

The objective of all of us in crafting this Bill is to increase the quantity and quality, and enhance the quality, of childcare available, to improve the well-being and prospects of our children and our society as a whole. Kinship care is an asset. We ought to think how we can engage that asset most effectively to achieve our overall objectives.

I can see that it would be inappropriate and political suicide for any government to insist that all kinship carers must conform to all the rules being laid down for professionals, but that does not mean they do not need and, in my view, deserve recognition and help if they want it. There are three kinds of care for children. There is parental care, which should remain at the core of the upbringing of children, with parents in control—unless the child is taken into care because the parents are unsuitable. There is already a duty for a local authority to support parents in their role as carers built in to this Bill. At the other end of the spectrum, statutory childcare—the main subject of the Bill—is provided for, regulated and inspected by the state. In between lies kinship care.

I should like to see the Bill provide for three things. The first is a recognition of the important role played by kinship care in local authority assessments of need under Clause 11 and in their plans for the provision of childcare facilities in their area under Clause 6. The second is a duty laid on local authorities to provide information, advice and training to kinship carers if requested by them to do so. The third is a duty laid on local authorities to provide assistance, including financial assistance, to kinship carers if they opt to become registered as providers of childcare and accept the requirements laid down by the Bill on providers of childcare. Is this covered by Clause 62? I think it is not. That section seems to be limited to providers of childcare, and that is the category from which kin are specifically excluded by the Bill.

In my view there are two reasons why these objectives are important. The first is to create a more level playing field between kinship care and state care. The second is to prevent a haemorrhaging of kinship carers from the system because they believe their services are no longer required. As the Bill is drafted, they would tend to presume that, as the local authority is obliged to provide affordable state childcare for all who need it, their services are no longer essential.

The Grandparents' Association has confirmed that many grandparents providing care are doing so at considerable sacrifice to themselves, but they believe it is necessary. I turn now to the effect of the amendments. On the duty to secure sufficient childcare, the effect would be to require local authorities to include family arrangements in the assessment and securing of childcare. We agree that for local authorities to establish parents' needs and identify gaps in the childcare market, they must assess the full extent of those needs, including any which are currently being filled by family carers purely because parents feel they have little choice of available childcare.

However, inclusion of family childcare in the duty under Clause 6 would also mean that local authorities would be required to secure sufficient of this type of childcare to meet parents' needs. That is clearly a nonsense in the circumstances since there is no way that the state can act to secure sufficient care by family members. The duties proposed for local authorities rightly, in our view, focus on formal, registered childcare to meet the needs of parents to enable them to work, or to train for work, and in particular those on low incomes or with disabled children. They deliberately do not extend into family provision. That is because we know that the dynamics of the childcare sector, with set-up and other costs, mean that formal childcare for these groups tends to be under-supplied and that meeting these needs will be vital in improving outcomes for those families who are most in need.

Amendment No. 70 follows that link, inserting two new paragraphs into Clause 41(3), which outlines the areas of learning and development requirements the Secretary of State must include in the early years foundation scheme. All the amendments highlight the significant differences in a child's growth between what others have referred to as the "baby" and "infant" years.

This builds on discussion during the passage of the Bill in the other place, and our belief that children from the age of birth to two have different needs and, as such, should be treated differently. In the other place our amendments referred to "babies" and "young children", but, after talking to experts in this field, we have amended our amendments, and our terminology now refers to "young children" and "very young children". Our primary concern to differentiate in these areas springs from our commitment to attachment theory, of which the noble Earl, Lord Listowel, spoke so convincingly earlier in Grand Committee. As my honourable friend Tim Loughton said in the other place, Conservative Members are great devotees of Sue Gerhardt's book, Why Love Matters: How Affection Shapes A Baby's Brain. If anyone wants it, I have a copy here.

Much work is now done by organisations such as the Oxford Parent-Infant Project. I declared an interest at Second Reading, but I declare it again. I am now a patron of OXPIP, which does a lot of work with social services to provide training in attachment, as well as a lot of research. One of its main areas of that research is to show that concentrating on babies, or very young children, from birth to two can make all the difference in the development of children and their chance of leading a fulfilling adult life.

On the first day of Committee, the noble Baroness, Lady Massey of Darwen, who is just walking away from her place, said that she had read some contradictory research about attachment theory, and that surely good-quality childcare is better than poor-quality homecare. No one could disagree. Poor-quality homecare is no respecter of money or education. A baby can have just as poor an attachment with a wealthy mother as with a mother from a deprived and struggling background, a mother with post-natal depression, or one who is a drug or substance abuser: all of them with their eyes blank and not available for their baby. It is vital that a baby receives consistent positive attention and care, either at home or in the nursery, but preferably in both. Good attachment means that the wiring of the baby's brain works, and the baby is able to look at the world and say, "This is actually a nice place".

If the baby is at a nursery, you have to look at how the baby is cared for there. Does the key worker have training in attachment theory? Do they attend to all the baby's needs? For instance, when the baby—the young child, rather; I am slipping back into our original terminology—is handed over at the beginning of the day, does the key worker change the baby's nappy, or do they change nappies on a rota system? When they interact with the baby, or the very young child, do they make and keep eye contact? All these things are vital for that very young child to attain the resilience that it is going to need throughout life.

Lord Adonis: These amendments reflect concerns about formalisation of early years and a well-founded desire, for all the reasons given by the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, to ensure that we appropriately support children in their earliest years and support a child's sense of self through secure attachments.

The early years foundation stage will have a framework capable of recognising and appropriately supporting the wide range of needs and abilities of all children in the early years, including the youngest ones. The aim of the early years foundation stage is to ensure that all children in early years settings are valued and supported through secure attachments with a key person for each child in each setting. This is crucial for children in their earliest years. Through that support, they will develop a strong sense of self and build strong self-esteem to enable them to use with confidence the skills that they are developing. A child's secure emotional development is intrinsic to the personal, social and emotional development area of learning in the early years foundation stage, which is already a part of this Bill.

I do not believe that there is a difference between the noble Baroness and myself on the objectives that we are seeking to secure. However, when it comes to the means to do so, we meet a parting of the ways. Amendment No. 57 would introduce the new legal concept of a "very young child" under the age of
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC316
 
between two and three, because of the dates set out in the amendments by which a child ceases to be "very young" and becomes "young"—and then will become less young thereafter. We think it would be unduly rigid to have such a legal demarcation, and indeed we fear it might work against the focus on the needs of the individual child, which is the objective that we are seeking to achieve and put at the heart of the early years foundation stage, and which has very wide support among the practitioner community. Indeed, that is a good part of the reason we are going down this road in the first place.

We support an approach without an unnecessary artificial divide, during which each individual child can develop at their own pace in a way that reflects their particular development needs and their achievements. For children this young, it is not appropriate to create an age-based split that ignores the child and attempts to define absolutely when a child should move from one phase to the next. That is more, rather than less, likely to lead to too much formalisation in learning—which is not the objective that I believe that the noble Baroness is keen to attain.

Young children need to be able to develop at their own pace. A child who develops quickly should not be held back simply because they are not old enough to move into the proposed two to five phase for young children. Neither should a child who takes more time to develop be forced into activities that are inappropriate simply because their birthday defines that it is time to move on.

We in the Committee all acknowledge that children grow rapidly in the early years but at differential rates. At 12 months they may start to walk; at 18 months they may be confident walkers and climbers. Before they are two, they may be good runners as well, but other children may not develop at the same rate. Similarly, some children have a wide vocabulary at 18 months and are starting to form proper sentences, while others are far from that stage. At two and a half, which is the upper end of the age range set by the noble Baroness in her "very young children" phase, the difference can be even more marked. We believe that we need a framework that supports staff in offering activities that are appropriate, recognises the needs of the individual child and does not hold children back, as we believe would be the effect of trying to separate out the two phases in the way suggested.

To support this, appropriate ongoing observation and assessment are crucial. The existing frameworks—that is, Birth to Three Matters and the foundation stage—highlight the importance of assessing children through ongoing observation. That is achieved through the normal course of a child's learning and play. The amendments would create a framework based neither on a child's level of development nor just on their age, but one based on their age and when they were born in the academic year. For instance, two children born on 2 September 2006 and 31 August 2007 respectively—I am not seeking to make a narrow point; there are a lot of children who were born on those dates—would both be moved into the two to five
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC317
 
phase envisaged in this amendment on 1 September 2009, even though one is only just two and the other is a day short of three, which at that stage of a child's life is a huge difference between them. That may be appropriate, but in many cases it will not. As I have said, we believe it is the child's individual level of development that should determine the activities provided, and that the existing arrangements in the Bill are best suited to ensuring that.

The early years foundation stage will of course allow differentiation between what is right and appropriate for babies and toddlers and for older children. It will do so in the context of an appropriate play-based approach, with a balance of adult-led and child-initiated activities. For instance, the existing Birth to Three Matters sets out that practitioners should use "gloop" in small trays so that babies can enjoy making marks in it.

The foundation stage, for slightly older children, sets out that practitioners should,

    "plan games to encourage children to move and then stop, for example moving like an animal".

The Bill contains provisions that will allow this ethos to continue. That will be fully reflected in the early years foundation stage, and we do not need to segment that stage in ways that could cause unnecessary rigidities.

I hope that I have clearly demonstrated why a split would not be appropriate, although it is perfectly reasonable for the noble Baroness to probe us in this area because the concerns on undue formalisation have been voiced outside.

4 pm

Amendment No. 70 would add to the areas of learning and development in Clause 41, the words,

    "the very young child's sense of self"

and "secure attachment".

I would like to make clear that we regard a strong sense of self and secure attachment as central to our approach in the early years and that they will continue to be central under the EYFS.

The EYFS document is being developed from an extensive range of research that identifies the most effective practice to support the best outcomes for children. The existing Birth to Three Matters framework makes clear that it is essential for each child to have a key person in the setting. The framework has been referred to in the supporting criteria of the national standards since October of last year. This will also be a learning and development requirement under the EYFS. It will highlight the importance of key workers and other early years practitioners forming warm relationships with babies and very young children. As a requirement of the EYFS, inspection of settings will include ensuring that each child has a key person and how this is effectively managed in supporting children's personal, social and emotional development in line with the EYFS.
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC318
 

In the Birth to Three Matters framework, "A Strong Child" features as a key aspect of learning. This focuses on children developing a sense of themselves and others, feeling self-assured and supported, valuing individuality and the contributions of themselves and others, and gaining self-assurance through a close relationship.

Similarly, under the existing foundation stage guidance, the personal, social and emotional development area of learning includes such matters as knowing who you are, where you fit in and feeling good about yourself. Early years providers need to support children to develop strong self-esteem to use the key skills that they are developing with enjoyment and confidence.

The aim of the early years foundation stage is to build on these frameworks to create coherence, avoid duplication, support the integration of care and education in the early years and allow children to develop at their own pace. We believe, therefore, that the objectives of the noble Baroness are secured without the need to segment the under-fives period as proposed in her amendments.

Baroness Crawley: We have had a short but important debate on the requirements to register. I am afraid that I will have to resist the amendments of the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley. There will not be a meeting of minds this afternoon on this.

Clause 96 defines early years provision as,

    "the provision of childcare for a young child".

The term "early years provider" therefore includes anyone who cares for a young child, from schools to nurseries, and from childminders to babysitters and nannies.

As the definition of early years provision is so wide, the Bill includes powers at Clauses 33(2) and 34(3) for the Secretary of State to exempt by order certain providers from the requirement to register. We have made a paper available to Members of the Committee to illustrate the types of childcare providers that we intend to exempt. This will include childcare provided in the child's own home and childcare provided only for short or infrequent periods of time. We have drawn the line carefully between what will and what will not be required to be registered to ensure that we put in place a regulatory regime that is proportionate and risk-based.

The effect of Amendments Nos. 63 and 65 would be to disapply the lists in Clauses 33(3) and 34(4) in respect of childcare which is provided on a regular basis. From the noble Baroness's remarks, it would appear that the intention is to remove the ability to exempt from registration any provision that is provided regularly. In addition, the definition of "regular" is not clear, and that would cause problems when this legislation is put into practice. For example, a half-term club that lasts for two days every year
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC323
 
would occur "regularly" but we would not require it to register because involving Ofsted in provision that is so short would not represent a proportionate approach.

As our paper shows, the Bill will enable us to draw the line for registration on the early years register in what we believe to be a sensible place. That will mean that we can use our resources where they can have the greatest effect—registering and inspecting provision which children attend for significant periods and which provides them with learning and development opportunities over a sustained period. We believe that the Bill reflects those priorities.

Providers such as nannies, of course, do care for children over sustained periods. 4 May 2006 : Column GC324
 

Registered providers on the OCR will be subject to registration requirements, which they will have to meet prior to being registered, as well as requirements set in regulations governing their ongoing activity. We want to consult to find the best way of designing the scheme. That means that we need the flexibility provided for by "may" in both sets of regulation-making powers to avoid lack of clarity and duplication of requirements.

An example is the complaints procedure that providers will be required to have. Having a complaints procedure could be either a registration requirement or a regulation governing the activity, and we want to consult about the most appropriate way of including it. One option would be for providers to have a complaints procedure that is checked at registration. Alternatively, the system may work better if providers are required to have a complaints procedure only once they are operational. The flexibility that the current drafting of these clauses provides enables us to design an optimal system. This flexibility would be removed if both sets of requirements had to include the things listed.

In addition, since these amendments refer to the registration requirements for the voluntary element of the scheme, but not the regulations governing activities for the compulsory element, if accepted we would not have a coherent system. We may have to develop different systems for the two elements, and that is not desirable.

My noble friend has provided a paper outlining our proposals for the Ofsted childcare register which demonstrate the areas that we propose should be covered. We attach importance to these matters and we hope that this will reassure noble Lords that the amendment will not be necessary.

Amendment No. 84 seeks to require all later years childcare which is required to be registered with Ofsted to face inspection at a period of not less than every three years. We fully understand the purpose behind this amendment—the desire to secure for children provision which is suitable, safe and of good quality. We all share that aim.

The Government are reforming and extending the regulatory framework for childcare. We are putting in place a system which is proportionate and risk-based where Ofsted's resources are directed to where they will have the greatest impact. That fits within the Government's drive to reduce the burden of inspection so that, where appropriate, funding can be directed more effectively to the front line. It also takes account of the growth and development of the childcare market over recent years.

Ofsted plays a vital role in ensuring that childcare provision is safe and suitable for children. Registration with Ofsted ensures providers meet specified standards and criteria. That will continue to be the case. In addition, we intend to require that every year providers will need to demonstrate to Ofsted that they continue to meet the registration criteria. We believe that this will ensure that provision continues to meet the standards for safety and suitability.
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC325
 

Ofsted will be able to inspect any provider on the Ofsted childcare register, and will do so where concern exists or a complaint has been received rather than on an automatic cycle. In this way attention and resource will be more focused to where it is needed. Automatic, periodic inspection of all registered provision would, on the other hand, incur high costs but there is no evidence of the benefits it would bring. I believe that there is some ambiguity over the wording of the amendment, with the danger that we would appear to be limiting inspection activity by preventing inspections from happening within an interval of three years. 4 May 2006 : Column GC328
 
here, announced the creation of a new early years professional role. That will be at graduate level, rather than level 3, as was previously suggested. That is very welcome. There will be a target of one EYP in every children's centre by 2010 and one in every full daycare setting by 2018. The EYP will not be the leader of a setting but will take "a professional leading role". That role is currently undertaken by teachers, who provide input into the settings' educational provision.

The action plan states that,

    "those most likely to want to take on the EYP role include teachers, community nurses, social workers and those with early years, childhood development and play qualifications".

There will, however be a range of training routes for those without such skills, knowledge and experience, with the emphasis on settings growing their own EYP. The action plan concludes that

    "it is important that this work proceeds in parallel with a wider debate about the longer-term relationship between the Early Years Professional and qualified teacher roles".

That statement appears to confirm the suspicion that the Government envisage the EYP role as replacing, rather than enhancing, the work of qualified teachers within the early years sector.

As the EYP would be equivalent to—but not actually be—a qualified teacher, they would not be subject to the school teachers' pay and conditions document provisions. There has been no suggestion that a national pay scale or conditions of service protocol will be developed by the Government. Therefore, teachers who took up employment in EYP roles would probably have to negotiate individual contracts of employment. That concerns the NUT, which is one of the organisations that has briefed me on the subject.

The EYP standards have been developed by the Children and Workforce Development Council, in conjunction with the Training and Development Agency for Schools. I accept that they draw heavily on the current trainee entry requirements for initial teacher training and the revised QTS standards.

However, a number of standards involve management responsibilities that are far more demanding than the QTS standards, although the two qualifications are described as equivalent. For example, section 3 of the EYP standards relates to leading practice and settings and to the leadership of the team to deliver the early years foundation stage. In addition, a number of the EYP standards are closely aligned to the professional activities covered by the QTS standards—for example, planning and leading learning activities; assessment monitoring and feedback; and reviewing and evaluating practice. Because of the close match between many of the QTS and EYP standards, it would not have been difficult to have developed an early years QTS training model, if the Government wanted to preserve the role of qualified teachers in the sector.

I wonder whether the Minister can tell us why the Government have not followed that route, despite the urgings of many experts in the field. One of them recently e-mailed me stating:
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC329

4.45 pm

I have also had support for this view from Professor Iram Siraj-Blatchford of the Institute of Education, who led on the qualitative aspects of the much respected EPPE research. She states:

I am getting slightly getting ahead of myself. Let me deal with Amendment No. 65ZA first. We agree that practitioners should be well trained and that settings should have to meet requirements in terms of qualifications. Research evidence tells us that the qualifications and training of childcare workers have a positive impact on children's outcomes. Specifically, the Effective Provision of Pre-school Education Project, referred to by the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley, tells us that children make more progress in settings where staff have higher qualifications, and that settings where trained teachers work with the children have the greatest impact on quality. I cannot stress too strongly that there is no plot, whatever the National Union of Teachers may say, by us to have qualified teachers playing a lesser role in early years settings than is the case today.

Indeed, if Members of the Committee would pay attention to my letter to the noble Lord, Lord Northbourne, of 2 May, which sets out the ratios and qualifications requirements we wish to see henceforth for children aged over three, they will see that there is a significant incentive for registered early years providers to recruit qualified teachers—that is those with a qualified teacher status. All such providers would then be able to take advantage of the ratios which currently pertain for three and four year-olds only in maintained nursery schools and nursery classes in maintained schools. Our reason for doing that is precisely because of the argument put forward by the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley, that when you have highly trained individuals leading those settings, it is appropriate that the ratios that currently apply in the case of maintained nursery schools and nursery classes could apply more widely. We have no intention whatever to change those ratios in respect of maintained nursery schools and nursery classes; namely, that there must be one member of staff for every 13 children. At least one member of staff must be
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC337
 
a school teacher, as defined by Section 122 of the Education Act 2002. I hope that goes some way to meeting her concerns on this.

In setting the ratio and qualification requirements, we have to strike a careful balance. As well as ensuring that provision is of a high quality, we need to ensure that we do not set that bar so high that existing settings, with which parents may be very happy, are not able to continue operating.

Our proposals will allow other early years settings the flexibility to meet the same ratio requirements of fewer adults to children as maintained nursery schools and classes for a limited period of the day if a graduate-level professional is present. For this "maintained nursery school" ratio to apply, the graduate-level professional would need to hold qualified teacher status, early years professional status or another suitable level 6 qualification, and we are giving significant incentives for the training of more level 6 qualified individuals with the transformation fund that I have referred to in almost every speech I have made from this Box in our proceedings.

The Children and Workforce Development Council has worked with a range of partners to develop a set of draft standards that describe the skills, knowledge and competencies that we believe are necessary for those who wish to gain early years' professional status.

5.15 pm

The draft standards are currently being consulted on and have been developed in line with revised standards for qualified teacher status but with a greater emphasis on the youngest children and child development, precisely to meet the concerns of the noble Earl, Lord Listowel. They therefore cover the nought to five age range more comprehensively than the QTS standards, which cover the teaching of children from age three upwards.

I shall give examples of the skills and knowledge that it will be important for early years professionals to have. They will need to focus on wider child development, as young children learn from everything they do, experience and observe. They will need to be able to gain knowledge not just of the child but also of the child's wider environment and those they are with outside the setting so that they can develop and nurture a dynamic relationship; and they will need strong communication skills so that they can work with families and other professionals to ensure a better experience for each child.

We will work with the Children and Workforce Development Council to determine what other level 6 qualifications may be appropriate for staff in this
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC338
 
role. Many vocations, including social work and play work, have a focus on working with children, and some level 6 qualifications associated with these vocations focus on working with young children and child development. The experience of such practitioners can enhance the overall experience of settings, individual children and families by helping to ensure a holistic approach.

The amendment would also require the Secretary of State to publish, and early years settings to have regard to, guidance on staffing matters. We agree that this is important and, as I have said, we propose to set qualification and ratio requirements as part of the early years foundation stage. In addition to setting requirements, the EYFS will provide guidance, for example, on exceeding the minimum ratio requirements when that is appropriate to meet the needs of individual children, on putting in place contingency arrangements to ensure that ratio requirements are met in the case of staff absence, and on the types of continuing professional development it is appropriate to offer staff so that they have the up-to-date skills and knowledge that they need to do their jobs properly.

I accept that the amendment is intended as a probing amendment on our intentions in respect of qualified teachers in early years settings, but I should add that it would remove the requirement that the Secretary of State specify welfare requirements as part of the early years foundation stage. It is through these welfare requirements that we propose to set the qualification and ratio requirements that I have just described, and if we did not have those it would mean that the EYFS would consist only of learning and development requirements. I do not think that that was in the mind of the noble Baroness who moved the amendment but I simply point it out in terms of drafting.

We now move on to the issue raised in a number of amendments which reflect concerns over the formalisation of the early years and seek to remove the word "taught". I hope that I can provide reassurance that with the early years foundation stage we will have a framework that is fully capable of recognising and appropriately supporting the wide range of needs and abilities of all children in the early years.

I emphasise strongly that the early years foundation stage will be based on children learning through play with enjoyment and challenge. Research tells us that the most effective settings use play to provide the basis of instructive learning. The document that I circulated earlier—the Direction of Travel Paper on the early years foundation stage—makes that very clear. Paragraphs 12 and 13 of the document state that,

    "EYFS will build on detailed research findings. The current Foundation Stage builds on the Effective Provision of Pre-school Education (EPPE) longitudinal research project and Researching Effective Pedagogy in the Early Years . . . both of which provide a significant body of research into effective early learning and development practice.

    EPPE concludes that in the most effective centres, 'play' environments were used to provide the basis of instructive learning. The most effective approach, and one which will be at the core of EYFS, is both 'teaching' and providing freely chosen yet potentially instructive play activities".


 
4 May 2006 : Column GC339
 

This makes explicit the objectives flagged up by Members of the Committee in terms of what will actually constitute effective early years provision and makes it clear that those are entirely shared by the Government.

However, learning through play does not remove the need for appropriate teaching through adults planning, joining in and extending play. The process of learning needs to be a joint enterprise between the child and the early years practitioner. The word "taught" is recognised by practitioners in the early years sector and is not about formalised, classroom-style teaching. It reflects the language already used in the foundation stage and, indeed, is used in the Education Act 2002 in almost precisely the way we use it here in respect of under-fives. It sets out in detail for the foundation stage what we mean by teaching for those aged from three to five, and states:

    "Teaching has many aspects, including planning and creating a learning environment, organising time and material resources, interacting, questioning, responding to questions, working with and observing children, assessing and recording children's progress, and sharing knowledge gained with other practitioners and parents".

In other words, it embraces all of the activities to which noble Lords drew attention in their contributions.

The Birth to Three Matters framework similarly reflects a wide definition and includes many examples of informal teaching. For example, under the section on "A skilful communicator", the framework suggests that practitioners should,

    "help children learn the names of other children and important people in their lives, for example through songs and rhymes".

Most people, and certainly most early years practitioners, would recognise this as teaching, although it is both age-appropriate and informal. I can assure Members of the Committee that we aim to build on the existing frameworks. We have no plans whatever to change this effective approach.

I turn finally to Amendment No. 69, spoken to by the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley. It seeks to ensure that the assessment of a child's progress is undertaken by observation. The noble Baroness elaborated the point in her second contribution. We agree that the most appropriate means of assessing young children is by observation. I can reassure noble Lords that this principle will be very firmly set out in the early years foundation stage document, as it was in the EYFS Direction of Travel document. The early years foundation stage will require those who plan and deliver early years provision to assess children through ongoing observation through the normal course of a child's learning and play. There will be no testing during this important stage of children's learning and development. However, the term "assessment" also incorporates the observations and contributions made by parents and other early years settings to inform a profile of the whole child. To say in the Bill that assessment has to be undertaken by observation would narrow the interpretation and not reflect the valuable contribution made by parents and other settings to a child's overall assessments.
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC340
 

I hope that I have provided reassurances for all Members of the Committee who have spoken in this debate. When we use the word "taught", we actually encompass all those things that they wish to see take place in early years settings.

Baroness Morris of Bolton: I thank the Minister for his reply. This is a good-natured Committee, but the Minister has said that we are not talking about the "nationalisation of childhood", and that I have spoken of "abolishing childhood". Lord Adonis: These amendments probe the issue of exemptions and modifications. Amendments Nos. 76 and 78 would require regulations to be made outlining a process whereby parents could appeal against a disapplication or modification of the learning and development requirements in respect of certain early years providers or of a particular child. The early years foundation stage may initially be challenging for some providers, particularly those who do not currently deliver the foundation stage. The content of the EYFS will be finalised this year, but until it is finalised and published and we have consulted on it, it will not be possible to specify in detail which parts providers may be exempted from or which modifications may be appropriate.

I can say that we envisage that exemptions or modifications in respect of providers should be made only in exceptional circumstances for a limited period to allow them time to improve their standards in order to meet the full requirements. During the period of such an exemption or modification, local authorities would be expected to use their powers under Clause 13 to support providers in improving the quality of provision. Supporting existing providers to improve will be a key priority for local authorities in advance of the full implementation of the EYFS in two years' time.

Turning to Amendment No. 78, we intend to design the EYFS to be flexible enough to meet the individual needs of all children and to accommodate the values
 
4 May 2006 : Column GC343
 
and philosophies of their families. However, it is difficult to foresee every circumstance that might arise. Our intention is not to allow providers to inappropriately exclude from provision children with disabilities or special educational needs—the concern rightly raised by the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley—but to produce sensible legislation with an appropriate degree of flexibility to enable settings to respond to exceptional circumstances.

We are mindful of the potential human rights implications and the right of parents to have their children educated in accordance with their own philosophies and beliefs. Clause 46 therefore ensures that settings have sufficient flexibility to deal with an exceptional circumstance or specific philosophy or belief that has not been anticipated. Without the clause, we believe there would be a legitimate concern that we were imposing an inflexible one-size-fits-all framework on families from all types of backgrounds and all types of religious and other beliefs, with no potential for taking account of an individual or local exceptional circumstance. We propose, as part of the specific circumstances of an exemption or modification in respect of an individual child, that this would be at the request of parents. We believe that there is therefore no need for parents to have a right of appeal.

Amendments Nos. 75 and 77 in the name of the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, seek to create a duty to set out and publish the reasons for modifying the EYFS or exempting a provider from delivering some aspect of it, whether those exemptions or modifications apply in relation to a provider, a group of providers or an individual child. The circumstances in which exemptions can be made will be set out in regulations and will be subject to full consultation and parliamentary scrutiny. If a group of providers is exempted, that will need to be set out and published so that those who are exempted know that they are. In the case of individual providers, however, we do not think it would be appropriate to publish the reasons in every circumstance. There will be clearly specified circumstances for individual providers and we will consider what steps may be appropriate to notify others, including parents, of the applied exemption or modification. A general duty to publish reasons in all cases would not be necessary.

In the case of disapplications or modifications in respect of individual children, it would be unfair to single out a particular child in such a way, especially as the reason may be of a sensitive nature. For example, it may relate to the religious beliefs of the child's family. We propose that such exemptions should be at the request of parents, which will be part of the specified circumstances as set down in the regulations. In view of those reassurances, I hope both noble Baronesses will not think their amendments necessary.

part of that time, no annual reports were produced. The commission was not engaged in any active distribution programmes during the periods in question and operates on a care and maintenance basis.

Sudan: Darfur

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Foreign and Commonwealth Office (Lord Triesman): I commend the tireless efforts of the African Union (AU) mediation in Abuja to reach a peace deal for Darfur and welcome its tabling, on 25 April, of compromise proposals for a Darfur peace agreement (DPA). My right honourable friend the Secretary of State for International Development (Mr Hilary Benn) has been at the Abuja talks since 2 May. He is supporting the AU in its efforts to get the parties to agree a deal.

I welcome the fact that the Government of Sudan are willing to sign the DPA. But I was extremely disappointed to hear that the rebel movements have rejected the first draft. They must realise that no deal will meet their full requirements; but this agreement is a fair and just one. It offers them participation in government and a political platform from which they can influence Darfur's future. I agree with the AU chief mediator's assessment that any future negotiations are likely to be less favourable to the movements.

The pressure is now on the rebel movements to agree the deal. The international community will not understand if they fail to take this opportunity to bring peace to Darfur and security to its people. We have already made it clear that those who impede the peace process will face sanctions under United Nations Security Council Resolution 1591. The rebel movements need to understand that this is their opportunity for a just settlement and such an opportunity is unlikely to recur.

Darfur now stands at a critical juncture. Its people have suffered for far too long and it rests on the parties to the Abuja talks to give them the opportunity to rebuild their lives.

Written Answers

Thursday, 4 May 2006.

Afghanistan: Helmand Province

Lord Astor of Hever asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Whether substantive and acceptable arrangements have been made with Pakistan to secure the overland supply routes into the British area of operations in the Helmand province of Afghanistan. [HL5483]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Ministry of Defence (Lord Drayson): Yes. The United Kingdom and Pakistan have agreed a memorandum of understanding to permit communications across Pakistani territory.

Armed Forces: HMS Collingwood Museum

Lord Astor of Hever asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What steps they are taking to support the museum of early naval communications technology at the HMS Collingwood Museum. [HL5377]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Ministry of Defence (Lord Drayson): The Ministry of Defence supports the museum by the provision of accommodation for it at the HMS Collingwood site and the payment of rates and associated utility bills. Maintenance of the equipment holdings is conducted by museum volunteers.

Armed Forces: Voter Registration

Lord Garden asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What arrangements have been put in place to facilitate voting by service personnel in Afghanistan and Iraq in the local elections on 4 May. [HL5345]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Constitutional Affairs (Baroness Ashton of Upholland): Service personnel serving overseas are able to vote by either post or proxy. In addition the Ministry of Defence, in association with the independent Electoral Commission, has been running a publicity campaign to inform service personnel of the voting arrangements. This includes a leaflet, outlining the respective voting options, and a postal and proxy vote application form being distributed to service personnel.

The Belarusian Ministry of Foreign Affairs has stated that the decision to implement the bans demonstrated the EU's failure,

    "to treat with respect the clearly expressed will of an independent nation",

and has threatened reciprocal measures.

The UK and the EU have consistently made it clear to the Belarusian authorities that we remain willing to discuss lifting the restrictive measures if there were sufficient improvements in the democratic and human rights situation as set out in the EU's benchmarks. The Council common position imposing the travel ban sets out clearly the actions that the Belarusian Government need to take in order for the ban to be reviewed.

Children: Health

4 May 2006 : Column WA97
 

Earl Howe asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What is the average volume of soft drinks consumed each day by children in England and Wales; and whether they will assess the implications for childhood obesity of the calorific content of such drinks; and [HL5480]

    Whether they will review possible action to reduce calorie levels in soft drinks through the wider use of calorie-free sweeteners as part of their strategy to halt the rise in childhood obesity. [HL5481]

Lord Warner: The most recent information available on consumption of soft drinks by children dates back to the 1997 national diet and nutrition survey of young people aged four to 18 years. This survey showed that average consumption of soft drinks by the four-to-18-year age group in Britain in 1997 was 441g/day. Just over half of this (264g/day) comprised standard (that is, other than low-calorie) varieties.

The causes of obesity are multifactorial. The Government have no current plans to assess the specific implications on childhood obesity of consumption of high-sugar soft drinks. Nor do they intend to promote the use of calorie-free sweeteners in soft drinks as part of their strategy to halt the rise in childhood obesity. The Food Standards Agency has commenced discussions with stakeholders on a strategy to address the calorie levels in foods to improve energy balance; the levels of sugar in some foods, including soft drinks, is being explored as part of this strategy.

Disarmament

Lord Jopling asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What are the issues on which a consensus agreement has not been reached thus holding up progress on United Nations disarmament agreements in Geneva; and which countries have blocked agreements to each of the issues. [HL5350]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Foreign and Commonwealth Office (Lord Triesman): The Conference on Disarmament (CD) in Geneva has been unable to reach consensus on a programme of work for more than eight years. During that time the only issue which all countries have agreed should be taken forward is a fissile material cut-off treaty (FMCT). The Government have called continuously for the immediate commencement of negotiations on an FMCT. Unfortunately, many other countries have
 
4 May 2006 : Column WA98
 
sought to link progress on an FMCT to progress on a range of other disarmament-related issues. This has led to the stalemate that we see today in the CD. As my honourable friend the Minister of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs, Kim Howells, said during his recent address to the CD in March this year, the package approach to starting substantive work in the CD has not delivered a consensus agreement on the way forward. But the CD is too important a mechanism to allow to remain inactive. It is therefore time to take a more pragmatic approach and start negotiating an FMCT.

EU: Relations with NATO

Lord Dykes asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What plans they have to discuss with the member states of the European Union the state of relations between NATO and the European Union. [HL5364]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Foreign and Commonwealth Office (Lord Triesman): The UK regularly discusses EU-NATO relations with other EU member states, both in EU meetings and bilaterally. Foreign Ministers of EU and NATO countries hold regular informal meetings, most recently on 27 and 28 April in Sofia. Under the Berlin plus arrangements, EU and NATO meetings also take place between the Political and Security Committee and the North Atlantic Council. At the technical level, the EU-NATO Capability Group provides a forum for the exchange of information on military research and procurement. The UK was one of the key advocates for the development of the permanent liaison arrangements to ensure transparency between the EU and NATO which were agreed in December 2005. The UK continues to press for a strong EU-NATO relationship to ensure the organisations are mutually reinforcing and prevent unnecessary duplication. There has also been effective practical co-operation between the organisations, for example, in Darfur where the EU and NATO provide complementary support to the African Union, and following Hurricane Katrina where NATO transported EU aid.

Foreign and Commonwealth Office: Staff

Lord Dykes asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Whether morale among senior Foreign and Commonwealth Office personnel has been adversely affected by recent reductions in budgets and posts, both abroad and at home. [HL5310]
     

4 May 2006 : Column WA99
 

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Foreign and Commonwealth Office (Lord Triesman): While we have closed some posts, this is in response to changing priorities, not overall budget reductions: the total Foreign and Commonwealth Office (FCO) departmental expenditure limit was increased in the 2004 spending review by £91 million in 2006-07 and £110 million in 2007–08. There is no evidence overall of a decline in morale occasioned by post closures, although some staff directly affected have expressed concerns. As measured by the staff survey published on 4 April 2006, which included senior staff, morale appears to be high: 77 per cent of FCO staff at home and abroad are proud to work for the FCO, 83 per cent feel they are doing something worth while and 91 per cent are prepared to put in extra effort when required. These figures compare well both to previous years and to other government departments. But the results also showed some areas we need to address, including recognition and reward of that commitment, leadership and how we manage and communicate change. We are taking action to tackle those issues. The results of the survey are available on the internet at the following address: www.civilservice.gov.uk/management/statistics/news/staff–survey/index.asp and will be made available to honourable Members in the Library of the House.

Policy on NI contributions rests with H M Revenue and Customs.

Passports

Lord Laird asked Her Majesty's Government:

    In what circumstances people born or resident in the Irish Republic may obtain a British passport; and [HL5181]
     

4 May 2006 : Column WA103
 

    In view of the concept of parity of esteem and equality outlined in the 1998 Belfast agreement, whether they intend to make arrangements to provide British passports on demand to residents of the Irish Republic. [HL5182]

Lord Bassam of Brighton: British passports are available to those who hold British nationality, which is a matter of law defined principally by the terms of the British Nationality Act 1981. This is not affected by the 1998 Belfast agreement.

Citizens of the Republic of Ireland who were, immediately before 1 January 1949, both citizens of Eire and British subjects may claim to remain British subjects, and therefore retain eligibility for British passports describing them as British subjects, on one or both the following grounds:

    having been in Crown service under the government in the United Kingdom;

    having associations with the United Kingdom or an overseas territory by way of descent, residence or otherwise.

In certain circumstances, British subjects who were born before 1 January 1949 in the part of Ireland which now forms the Republic of Ireland but did not become Irish citizens would have become British citizens under the 1981 Act.

From 1 January 1949 onwards, British nationality has not been acquired by birth in the Republic of Ireland. Residents of the Republic of Ireland who are eligible for British passport facilities should apply to the British embassy.

Trade: ACP Countries

Lord Dykes asked Her Majesty's Government:

    In view of the progress of the World Trade Organisation's international trade liberalisation measures, when they next plan to raise in the European Union Council of Ministers relations between the European Union and the African, Caribbean and Pacific group. [HL5308]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Foreign and Commonwealth Office (Lord Triesman): The UK remains committed to an ambitious, broad-based and pro-development outcome to the World Trade Organisation's (WTO) round of trade negotiations. It is unfortunate that WTO members were unable to meet the end of April deadline for agreeing cuts in tariffs for industrial goods and agricultural products. But we will continue to push for a deal that can be finalised this year and which takes into account the needs of the poorest countries.

We understand the concerns from African, Caribbean and Pacific (ACP) countries about possible negative short-term impacts of trade liberalisation. However, these are outweighed by the potential benefits on offer from the trade round and from which
 
4 May 2006 : Column WA106
 
we all stand to gain. As set out in the Government's 2004 White Paper on trade and investment, the international response needs to acknowledge and tackle the short-term transition costs and look at how we can provide guaranteed, credible and additional aid to support these countries in the transition to more productive sectors.

We continue to argue for an effective response to the concerns of the ACP, including the provision of aid for trade and other transitional assistance for developing countries, and for economic partnership agreements with the EU to have a pro-development focus.

Specifically regarding the impact of EU sugar reform, my honourable friend the Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State for International Development, Gareth Thomas, raised the issue with other EU Development Ministers at the 10 and 11 April General Affairs and External Relations Council. We will continue to lobby the Commission and our EU partners on this specific issue and on wider ACP concerns about the impact of trade liberalisation.

Women and Equality Unit

Lord Ouseley asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What links have been established by the women's equality unit with the ethnic minority communities. [HL5394]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department of Trade and Industry (Lord Sainsbury of Turville): The Women and Equality Unit maintains a continuous and open dialogue with ethnic minority communities in close collaboration with the officials from the Home Office.

In particular, ministerial colleagues from the DTI and Home Office wrote on 18 March to a cross-section of black and minority ethnic community representatives, inviting them to join a new race equality group. This group will advise on both the work on the Discrimination Law Review and the transitional arrangements and priorities for race equality and race relations, within the context of the establishment of the Commission for Equality and Human Rights (CEHR).

It is hoped that the first meeting of this group will take place in May. Both Ministers and officials will participate in the work of this group.
